⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفهوم وأقسامه
(۱) قوله : " في كلام الشارع فقط استدل الحنفية على أن مفاهيم المخالفة في النصوص الشرعية في كتاب الله وسنة رسوله ليست بحجة بالأدلة التالية :
١ - إن فوائد القيود التي يقيد بها اللفظ كثيرة، فإذا ورد قيد منها في كلام الشارع ولم تظهر له فائدة معينة، فلا نستطيع أن نحكم بأن الفائدة لذلك القيد هي تخصيص الحكم بالمنطوق، ونفيه عما لا قيد فيه ؛ لأن مقاصد الشرع لا يمكن الإحاطة بها، بخلاف مقاصد البشر وأغراضهم، فإنه يمكن الإحاطة بها، ولهذا كان مفهوم المخالفة في كلام البشر معتبراً .
-۲- لم يعمل بمفهوم المخالفة فى كثير من الآيات القرآنية والأحاديث النبوية؛ إذ لو عمل به لأدت هذه النصوص إلى معان فاسدة أو إلى أحكام تنافي المقرر شرعا، مثل قوله تعالى : إن عدة الشهور عند الله اثنا عشر شهراً في كتاب الله يوم خلق السموات والأرض، منها أربعة حرم لم يكن التخصيص بالأربعة الحرم (رجب) وذو القعدة وذو الحجة والمحرم) دليلا على إباحة الظلم في غيرها من الأشهر ؛ لأن الظلم حرام فى جميع الأوقات.
-٣- لو كان مفهوم المخالفة معتبراً لما احتيج إلى النص عليه صراحة، كما في قوله تعالى : ولا تقربو هن حتى يطهرن، فإذا تطهرن فأتوهن من حيث أمركم الله وكما في قوله سبحانه: ﴿وربائبكم اللاتى فى حجوركم من نسائكم اللاتي دخلتم بهن فإن لم تكونوا دخلتم بهن فلا جناح عليكم ففي الآيتين نص الله سبحانه على حكم المسكوت عنه أصول الفقه الإسلامي للزحيلي : ٣٦٨/١،
(٣٦٩
وقوله : "فقط" أفاد هذا اللفظ أنه فى الروايات الفقهية ونحوها معتبر بأقسامه حتى مفهوم اللقب، كقولك : صلاة الجمعة على الرجال الأحرار، فيفهم منه عدم وجوبها على النساء والعبيد، كذا في رد المحتار : ۱۱۰/۱ .

مواضع اعتبار المفهوم
مطلب
المفهوم معتبر في كلام الناس
والعقليات والمعاملات لا في كلام الشارع
#
قال في شرح التحرير بعد قوله : غير معتبر في كلام الشارع فقط " : فقد نقل الشيخ جلال الدين الخبازى فى حاشية "الهداية" عن شمس الأئمة الكردرى : أن تخصيص الشيء بالذكر لا يدل على نفى الحكم عما عداه في خطابات الشارع . فأما في متفاهم الناس وعرفهم، وفي المعاملات، والعقليات - يدل . اهـ . وتداوله المتأخرون، وعليه ما في خزانة الأكمل " و" الخانية " . لو قال : " مالك على أكثر من مائة درهم" ، كان إقراراً بالمائة، ولا يشكل عليه عدم لزوم شيء في "مالك على أكثر من مائة درهم ولا أقل"، كما لا يخفى على المتأمل (۱) . اهـ .
(۱) قوله : "كما لا يخفى على المتأمل وجه عدم لزوم شيء في هذه الصورة أن من شروط اعتبار المفهوم أن لا يعارضه المنطوق، كما ذكر فى المصرع الثاني من البيت السابق، وههنا لفظ " ولا أقل" يعارض ما فهم من صدر الكلام.
Concept and its divisions
(1) His statement: "only in the speech of the Lawgiver" – The Hanafis argued that counter-concepts (mafahim al-mukhalafah) in the divine texts of Allah's Book and His Messenger's Sunnah are not valid proofs (hujja), with the following evidences:
1. The benefits of the qualifications by which a word is restricted are many. When a qualification occurs in the speech of the Lawgiver and no specific benefit for it appears, we cannot judge that the benefit of that qualification is to particularize the ruling to the explicit and negate it from what is not so qualified; because the purposes of the Law cannot be fully comprehended, unlike the purposes and aims of humans, which can be comprehended. Hence, counter-concept is considered valid in human speech.
2. Counter-concept was not applied in many Quranic verses and Prophetic hadith; for if it were applied, these texts would lead to corrupt meanings or rulings contradictory to established Shari'a. For example, Allah's statement: "Indeed, the number of months with Allah is twelve months in the book of Allah, on the day He created the heavens and the earth; of them, four are sacred" – the specification of the four sacred months (Rajab, Dhul-Qa'dah, Dhul-Hijjah, Muharram) does not prove the permissibility of injustice in other months, because injustice is forbidden at all times.
3. If counter-concept were considered valid, there would be no need to explicitly state the ruling in cases like Allah's words: "And do not approach them until they purify. Then when they have purified, come to them from where Allah has commanded you" and His saying: "And your stepdaughters who are in your care from your wives whom you have entered with; but if you have not entered with them, there is no sin upon you." In both verses, Allah explicitly stated the ruling for the unmentioned case. (Usul al-Fiqh al-Islami by al-Zuhayli: 1/368-369)
And his statement: "only" indicates that in juridical reports and similar texts, it is considered valid in all its divisions, even the concept of the label (mafhum al-laqab). For example, your saying: "Jumu'a prayer is obligatory upon free men" implies that it is not obligatory upon women and slaves. So in Radd al-Muhtar: 1/110.

Places where concept is considered

Issue: Concept is considered in the speech of people and in intellectual matters and transactions, not in the speech of the Lawgiver.

He said in Sharh al-Tahrir after his statement: "It is only not considered in the speech of the Lawgiver": Shaykh Jalal al-Din al-Khabbazi quoted in his gloss on al-Hidayah from Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari: that specifying something by mention does not indicate the negation of the ruling from what is other than it in the addresses of the Lawgiver. As for the mutual understanding of people and their custom, and in transactions and intellectual matters – it does indicate. End quote. The later scholars transmitted this, and upon it is what is in Khizanat al-Akmal and al-Khaniyya: If someone says: "I owe you more than one hundred dirhams," it is an acknowledgment of one hundred, and it is not problematic that nothing is due in "I owe you more than one hundred dirhams and not less" – as is not hidden from the one who reflects (1). End.
(1) His statement: "as is not hidden from the one who reflects" – the reason for nothing being due in this case is that among the conditions for considering the concept is that it should not be contradicted by the explicit wording, as mentioned in the second line of the previous couplet. Here, the phrase "and not less" contradicts what was understood from the beginning of the speech.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage articulates a fundamental rasm al-mufti principle: the Hanafi position that mafhum al-mukhalafah (the opposite implication derived from a qualified statement) is not a valid proof (hujja) when found in the speech of the Lawgiver (Quran and Sunnah). The author begins by stating the Hanafi argument with three evidences. First, the purposes of Allah are inscrutable; a qualification may serve multiple functions unknown to us, so we cannot confidently infer negation elsewhere. Second, applying mafhum al-mukhalafah to verses like the four sacred months would produce absurd or un-Islamic conclusions (e.g., permitting injustice in other months). Third, the Quran itself often explicitly states the ruling for the unmentioned case, rendering the concept unnecessary. The passage then clarifies that this rule is restricted: mafhum is considered valid in human speech, intellectual matters, and transactions, because human purposes are knowable. This distinction is illustrated with an example from Hanafi fiqh: if someone says 'I owe you more than one hundred dirhams,' it is considered an acknowledgment of exactly one hundred, because in human understanding the phrase implies that the minimum is one hundred. However, if he adds 'and not less,' the explicit statement contradicts the implied negation, so nothing is established. This lesson is crucial for muftis to avoid deriving rulings from mere textual implications in divine speech.

Key Terms
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafah): The opposite implication deduced from a qualified statement, e.g., 'if Zaid comes, honor him' implies 'if he does not come, do not honor him'.
مفهوم اللقب (mafhum al-laqab): Implication from a proper name or label; e.g., 'Jumu'a is upon free men' implies it is not upon women or slaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, why is mafhum al-mukhalafah not a proof in the speech of the Lawgiver?
A) Because Allah's purposes are fully comprehensible to humans.
B) Because applying it would always lead to correct rulings.
C) Because the Quran and Sunnah already contain explicit statements for every case.
D) Because the purposes of the Lawgiver cannot be fully grasped, and applying it sometimes leads to corrupt meanings.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, mafhum al-mukhalafah is only a valid proof in human speech and transactions, not in the Quran and Sunnah.

Where Mafhum Is Authoritative: Speech of People vs. Speech of the Lawgiver
States where mafhum is authoritative and where it is not, with proofs and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contexts where mafhum al-mukhalafa is considered authoritative in Hanafi usul and apply the rule that it is not considered in the speech of the Lawgiver (Qur'an and Sunnah).

Core Idea
Mafhum al-mukhalafa is authoritative in human speech, transactions, and rational matters, but not in Divine speech due to its concise nature.

The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between the speech of the Lawgiver (kalam al-shari') and the speech of ordinary people and transactions when it comes to the authority of implied meanings (mafhum). Understanding where mafhum applies is crucial for a mufti to correctly interpret texts, especially when deriving rulings from non-sacred sources like contracts, acknowledgments, and jurisprudential narrations. This lesson unpacks the key rule and its practical furu'.

Guided Path
1
1. State the core rule
Identify the main maxim: 'al-mafhum mu'tabar fi kalam al-nas wa al-mu'amalat wa al-'aqliyyat la fi kalam al-shari''. Understand that mafhum al-mukhalafa is presumed to be intended in ordinary human speech and transactions, but not in the Qur'an and Sunnah.
The exception is based on the nature of Divine speech as jawami' al-kalim.
2
2. Identify the proof for the exception
Locate the transmitted authority: Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari stated that specification by mention in Divine addresses does not imply negation of the ruling from what is omitted. This is the foundational proof for the rule.
Recall that the Hanafis rely on this transmission alongside the linguistic analysis of Divine speech.
3
3. Apply the rule to the furu' from Iqrar (acknowledgments)
Work through the two examples: (a) 'More than one hundred' → mafhum gives an acknowledgment of one hundred. (b) 'More than one hundred nor less' → the explicit 'nor less' contradicts the mafhum, so no binding acknowledgment.
The condition for mafhum is that it not be contradicted by mantuq (explicit wording).
4
4. Extend the rule to statements of Companions and narrations
Note that mafhum is considered in Companion statements by consensus, but only if the statement is based on opinion (ra'y), not if it is marfu' (raised to the Prophet). Similarly, mafahim al-kutub (concepts from madhhab books) are authoritative, while mafahim al-nusus (concepts from Qur'an and hadith) generally are not.
This distinction prevents the application of mafhum to sacred texts where it would create incorrect rulings.
5
5. Confirm the conditions and istithna'
Review the condition (shart) that mafhum must not be contradicted by mantuq, and the istithna' (exception) of kalam al-shari'. Understand that the exception is narrow and well-defined.
The istithna' is based on the unique characteristic of Divine speech.

المفهوم معتبر في كلام الناس
والعقليات والمعاملات لا في كلام الشارع

Topic: The concept (mafhum) is considered (authoritative) in the speech of people, rational matters, and transactions, but not in the speech of the Lawgiver. # He said in Sharh al-Tahrir after his saying: 'not considered in the speech of the Lawgiver only': 'Shaykh Jalal al-Din al-Khabbazi transmitted in the gloss of al-Hidayah from Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari that specifying a thing by mention does not indicate the negation of the ruling from what is other than it in the addresses of the Lawgiver. As for the mutual understanding of people and their custom, and in transactions, and rational matters – it does indicate. End. And the later scholars circulated it, and upon it is what is in Khizanat al-Akmal and al-Khaniyyah: If he said, 'You have a right upon me of more than one hundred dirhams,' it is an acknowledgment of one hundred, and it is not problematic regarding the non-binding of something in 'You have a right upon me of more than one hundred dirhams nor less' – as is not hidden from the one who reflects. (1) End. (1) His saying: 'as is not hidden from the one who reflects' – the reason for the non-binding of something in this form is that among the conditions for considering the concept is that it not be contradicted by the explicit statement, as mentioned in the second verse of the previous couplet, and here the word 'nor less' contradicts what was understood from the beginning of the speech. Topic: The concept is considered in the statements of the Companions, jurisprudential narrations, and rational causes. And in the margin of al-Nahr: The concept is considered in the narrations by consensus, and from it (1) are the statements of the Companions. He said: It should be restricted (2) to what is perceptible by opinion (3), not what is not perceptible by it. End. That is, because the statement of a Companion, if it is not perceptible by opinion, i.e., by ijtihad, it has the ruling of marfu' (raised to the Prophet), thus it is from the speech of the Lawgiver, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and the concept is not considered in it. So the meaning of 'narrations' is what is narrated in the books from the mujtahids among the Companions and others. And in al-Nahr also at the chapter on the Sunnas of wudu': 'The concepts of books are authoritative (hujja) contrary to most concepts of the texts (nusus).' (5) End. (1) His saying: 'from it' i.e., from what its concept is unanimously considered. (Mazhari) (2) His saying: 'it should be restricted' i.e., what was mentioned of considering the concept in the statements of the Companions. (Mazhari) (3) His saying: 'to what is perceptible by opinion' i.e., what the intellect has scope and disposal over. (Mazhari) (4) His saying: 'the concepts of books are authoritative' i.e., in narrations and the like, and are considered in all their divisions, even the concept of the title. (Mazhari) (5) His saying: 'contrary to most concepts of the texts like verses and hadith; because they are of concise speech (jawaami' al-kalim), so they carry many benefits and necessitate the specification of the explicit by mention – thus you see later scholars derive from them what earlier scholars did not grasp, contrary to narrations, for rarely do differences of viewpoints occur in them. What is meant is concepts of opposition (mafahim al-mukhalafa); as for concepts of agreement (mafahim al-muwafaqa), they are considered absolutely. And it is only restricted by 'most' because among the texts are those whose concept is considered, such as 'the punishment of the fire' (nahy al-uquba). (Mazhari)
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Rasm al-Mufti addresses the legal weight of mafhum (implicit meaning or concept of opposition) in different contexts. It begins by stating a fundamental rule: mafhum is authoritative in the speech of people, rational matters, and transactions, but not in the speech of the Lawgiver (i.e., Qur'an and Sunnah). This is a distinction based on the nature of Divine speech, which is concise and comprehensive (jawaami' al-kalim), and thus a specification by mention does not necessarily imply negation of the ruling from what is omitted. The author supports this by quoting Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari via Sharh al-Tahrir and then gives a practical example: if someone says 'I owe you more than one hundred dirhams,' it is an acknowledgment of exactly one hundred dirhams because the explicit wording implies that. But if he adds 'nor less,' the explicit statement ('nor less') overrides the mafhum from the first part, so no acknowledgment is binding. The commentary (footnote) explains that this is because a condition for considering mafhum is that it not be contradicted by the explicit statement (mantuq).

Then the passage extends the discussion to statements of the Companions and jurisprudential narrations. It states that mafhum is considered in the sayings of the Companions by consensus, but only those sayings that are based on opinion (ra'y) and ijtihad. If the Companion's saying is not based on opinion (i.e., it is a report from the Prophet, marfu'), then it has the status of the Lawgiver's speech, and mafhum is not considered. Similarly, in jurisprudential narrations (riwayat from mujtahids), mafhum is authoritative. The passage also distinguishes between 'concepts of books' (mafahim al-kutub, which are the books of the madhhab's jurists) and 'concepts of texts' (mafahim al-nusus, i.e., Qur'an and hadith). The former are authoritative even for mafhum al-mukhalafa, while the latter (texts) generally are not, except in certain cases like the verse about punishment (perhaps referring to the concept of 'punishment of the fire' implying that there is a punishment). The footnote clarifies that mafhum al-muwafaqa (concept of agreement, a fortiori) is authoritative absolutely in all contexts.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning derived from the wording, particularly mafhum al-mukhalafa (concept of opposition) that the opposite of the stated condition/description is negated.
المنطوق (al-mantuq): The explicit wording or what is directly stated.
كلام الشارع (kalam al-shari'): Speech of the Lawgiver, i.e., Qur'an and Sunnah, which is characterized by conciseness and comprehensiveness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is mafhum al-mukhalafa not considered in the speech of the Lawgiver (Qur'an and Sunnah)?
A) Because it is not transmitted reliably.
B) Because Divine speech is concise (jawaami' al-kalim) and specification does not imply negation.
C) Because the Lawgiver explicitly forbade using mafhum.
D) Because mafhum is only applicable to transactions.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-mukhalafa is authoritative in human speech and transactions but not in the speech of the Lawgiver; this distinction is fundamental to Hanafi textual interpretation.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
12 min
Consideration of Mafhum in Hanafi Usul al-Iftaa
Clarifying when mafhum is considered in Hanafi rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when mafhum is considered in Hanafi usul al-iftaa and apply the distinction between Shariah texts and narrations/rational causes.

Core Idea
Mafhum is valid in narrations and rational causes, but not in direct Shariah texts.

The concept of mafhum (implied meaning) is a critical tool in legal reasoning, but its validity varies by context. In this lesson, we examine a key passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti that delineates exactly where Hanafi scholars accept or reject arguments based on mafhum. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti to correctly derive rulings from source materials.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts for mafhum
From the examples in Ghayat al-Bayan, note that specification in narrations (riwayat) negates what is omitted, while in Shariah texts (nusus) it does not.
The first example: 'A woman does not have to undo her braids' implies a man does have to – accepted because it's a narration.
2
Distinguish between types of mafhum
Recognize the difference between mafhum al-muwafaqah (analogical implication) and mafhum al-mukhalafah (a contrario implication). The later scholars resolve the dispute by limiting acceptance to mafhum al-muwafaqah in narrations.
Mafhum al-mukhalafah from Shariah texts is rejected.
3
Analyze the rational analogy (Umar's case)
Examine how Umar's statement 'We initiated it' is used as a rational cause (illah aqliyyah) to deduce that if no initiation, no penalty. This shows mafhum is considered for rational causes.
Rational causes are not the speech of the Lawgiver, so their mafhum is valid.
4
Reconcile the apparent contradiction in the school
Compare statements from al-Ashbah (absolute rejection) and al-Siyar al-Kabir (acceptance). The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) reconcile by restricting rejection to Shariah texts and accepting mafhum al-muwafaqah in other contexts.
Imam Muhammad's use of mafhum is interpreted as mafhum al-mukhalafah, which is rejected.
5
Formulate the mufta bihi rule
The relied-upon rule: A mufti may not use mafhum al-mukhalafah from a Shariah text but may use mafhum from a narration or rational cause if it is mafhum al-muwafaqah.
An exception is made for endowment deeds (waqifin).

شرح عقود رسم المفتی مواضع اعتبار المفهوم وفي "غاية البيان" عند قوله (۱) : " وليس على المرأة أن تنقض ضفائرها "، احترز بالمرأة عن الرجل . وتخصيص الشيء في الروايات يدل على نفي ما عداه بالاتفاق، بخلاف النصوص فإن فيها لا يدل على نفى ما عداه عندنا (٢) . اهـ . وفي غاية البيان " أيضًا في باب جنايات الحج عند قوله (۳): "وإذا صال السبع على المحرم فقتله، لا شيء عليه؛ لما روى أن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه قتل سبعا وأهدى كبشاً ، وقال : إنا ابتدأنا (٤) . علل لإهداءه بابتداء نفسه، فعلم به أن المحرم إذا لم يبتدئ بقتله بل قتله دفعاً لصولته، لا يجب عليه شيء، وإلا لم يبق للتعليل فائدة . ولا يقال : تخصيص الشيء بالذكر لا يدل على نفى ما عداه عندكم فكيف تستدلون بقول عمر رضى الله تعالى عنه؟ لأنا نقول : ذاك في خطابات الشرع، أما في الروايات والمعقولات فيدل، وتعليل عمر من باب المعقولات" . اهـ . وحاصله : أن التعليل للأحكام تارة يكون بالنص الشرعي من آية أو حديث، وتارة يكون بالمعقول كما هنا. والعلل العقلية ليست من كلام الشارع فمفهومها معتبر ، ولهذا تراهم يقولون: مقتضى هذه العلة جواز كذا وحرمته " ، فيستدلون بمفهومها . مطلب في ذكر قولي الحنفية في المفهوم والتطبيق بينهما فإن قلت : قال في "الأشباه" من كتاب القضاء: "لا يجوز الاحتجاج بالمفهوم في كلام الناس في ظاهر المذهب كالأدلة، وأما مفهوم الرواية (۳) فحجة، كما في غاية البيان " من الحج" . اهـ . فهذا مخالف لما مر من أنه غير معتبر في كلام الشارع فقط . قلت : الذي عليه المتأخرون ما قدمناه . وقال العلامة البيري في شرحه : والذي في "الظهيرية " : "الاحتجاج بالمفهوم لا يجوز " . وهو ظاهر المذهب عند علمائنا - رحمهم الله تعالى- وما ذكره محمد في السير الكبير " من جواز الاحتجاج بالمفهوم، فذلك خلاف الظاهر . وأجيب بأنه : من باب حمل المطلق على المقيد . وأشار إلى ذلك في "حواشي الكشف " و "المصفق " و "خزانة الروايات" و "السراجية" . وخلاصة الجواب : أن المراد من المفهوم فيما تقدم من كلام الفقهاء هو المفهوم الموافق . والمنقول في "السير الكبير " هو المفهوم المخالف . اهـ . (۱) قوله : "لا يجوز الاحتجاج " قال الحموي في حاشيته : ينبغي أن يستثنى من ذلك عبارة الواقفين، فإنه يحتج بمفاهيمه . (مظ) (۲) قوله : "وأما مفهوم الرواية " أي الفقهية. وقوله : " ما قدمناه أى من عدم اعتباره في كلام الشارع واعتباره في غيره، ثم استدل عليه بكلام البيرى الذى يشتمل على تأييدات من السير الكبير " و "الظهيرية " و حواشي الكشف" و "المصفى" و "خزانة الروايات " و "السراجية"، ولكن لما كان ظاهر كلام البيرى مقتضيا لاعتباره مطلقا حتى في كلام الشارع، ولم يذهب إليه أحد، استدرك عليه بقوله : " أي إن العمل على جواز الاحتجاج بالمفهوم لكن لا مطلقاً بل في كلام الشارع " . فافهم .
Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti - Places where the implied meaning (mafhum) is considered. And in 'Ghayat al-Bayan' at his statement: 'A woman does not have to undo her braids,' he excluded the man by specifying the woman. Specifying something in narrations indicates the negation of what is other than it by agreement, contrary to the [direct] texts, for in them it does not indicate the negation of what is other than it according to us. End. And in 'Ghayat al-Bayan' also in the chapter of Hajj penalties at his statement: 'If a predator attacks a Muhrim and he kills it, there is nothing upon him; because it is narrated that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) killed a predator and sacrificed a ram, and said: We initiated it.' He gave the reason for his sacrifice as his own initiation, so it is known that if the Muhrim did not initiate the killing but killed it to repel its attack, nothing is incumbent on him; otherwise there would be no benefit in the reasoning. And it cannot be said: Specifying a thing by mention does not indicate the negation of what is other than it according to you, so how do you deduce from the statement of Umar? Because we say: That is in the addresses of the Shariah, but in narrations and rational matters it indicates, and Umar's reasoning is of the category of rational matters. End. The summary: That the reasoning for rulings is sometimes by the Shariah text from a verse or hadith, and sometimes by rational reasoning as here. Rational causes are not from the speech of the Lawgiver, so their mafhum is considered, and for this reason you see them saying: The requirement of this cause is permissibility of such and such and its prohibition, so they deduce by its mafhum. A section on mentioning the two statements of the Hanafis on mafhum and reconciling between them. If you say: He said in 'al-Ashbah' from the book of judiciary: 'It is not permissible to argue by mafhum in people's speech in the apparent madhhab, like the legal evidences. But the mafhum of the narration is a proof, as in 'Ghayat al-Bayan' from Hajj.' End. This contradicts what passed that it is not considered only in the speech of the Lawgiver. I say: What the later scholars are upon is what we have presented. And Allama al-Biri said in his commentary: What is in 'al-Zuhairiyya': 'Arguing by mafhum is not permissible.' And that is the apparent madhhab according to our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them). And what Muhammad mentioned in 'al-Siyar al-Kabir' about the permissibility of arguing by mafhum, that is contrary to the apparent. And it was answered that it is from the category of carrying the absolute to the restricted. And that was indicated in 'Hawashi al-Kashf', 'al-Misfa', 'Khazinah al-Riwayat', and 'al-Sirajiyya'. The summary of the answer: That what is meant by mafhum in what preceded from the jurists' speech is mafhum al-muwafaqah (analogical implication). And what is transmitted in 'al-Siyar al-Kabir' is mafhum al-mukhalafah (a contrario implication). End. (1) His statement: 'It is not permissible to argue' - al-Hamawi said in his hashya: An exception to that is the statements of endowers (waqifin), for one does argue by their mafhum. (Maz) (2) His statement: 'But the mafhum of the narration' i.e., of jurisprudence. And his statement: 'What we have presented' i.e., of not considering it in the speech of the Lawgiver and considering it in other than that. Then he adduced evidence for it from the statement of al-Biri which includes confirmations from 'al-Siyar al-Kabir', 'al-Zuhairiyya', 'Hawashi al-Kashf', 'al-Misfa', 'Khazinah al-Riwayat', and 'al-Sirajiyya'. But when the apparent meaning of al-Biri's statement required considering it absolutely even in the speech of the Lawgiver, and no one held that, he corrected it with his statement: 'That is, the practice is on the permissibility of arguing by mafhum, but not absolutely, rather in [other than] the speech of the Lawgiver.' So understand.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage from 'Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti' is a methodological ruling (rasm rule) addressing the consideration of mafhum (implied meaning) in Hanafi usul al-iftaa. The author, Ibn Nujaym, clarifies two key contexts where mafhum is accepted and where it is not. The first example from 'Ghayat al-Bayan' shows that specifying the woman in the ruling about undoing braids for wiping during wudu indicates that a man is not obligated to undo his braids. This is consistent with the principle that in narrations (riwayat), specification negates what is not specified by agreement. However, in direct scriptural texts (nusus) from the Quran and Sunnah, specification does not negate what is omitted, because the text only addresses the specified case and does not legislate for the omitted one. The second example from Hajj penalties illustrates that the mafhum of a rational analogy (alilla aqliyya) is considered. The case: 'Umar killed a predator while in ihram and offered a sacrificial animal, reasoning that he initiated the aggression. By analogy, if the animal initiated, no penalty is due. Even though the text specifying initiation might not negate non-initiation in Shariah texts, the narration is a rational statement and its mafhum is valid. The passage then addresses an apparent contradiction: 'Al-Ashbah' states that arguing by mafhum is not permissible in people's speech, while earlier it was stated that mafhum is considered in narrations. The resolution offered by later scholars (mutakhkhirin) is that mafhum is not considered only in the direct speech of the Lawgiver (Shariah texts), but it is considered in other contexts like narrations and rational statements. Al-Biri notes that the dominant Hanafi position, as in 'al-Zuhairiyya', is that arguing by mafhum is not permissible absolutely. However, Imam Muhammad's use of mafhum in 'al-Siyar al-Kabir' is reconciled by carrying the absolute to the restricted: the earlier jurists' use of mafhum refers to mafhum al-muwafaqah (analogical implication), while Imam Muhammad's use refers to mafhum al-mukhalafah (a contrario implication), which is not accepted in the madhhab. Footnotes provide additional nuance, including an exception for endowment deeds. The core rule for the mufti: In issuing fatwas, rely on explicit texts; mafhum is only used in non-Shariah contexts or when supported by qiyas.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning or inferred ruling from a statement, as opposed to the explicit wording (mantuq).
الروايات (al-riwayat): Jurisprudential narrations or transmitted statements from early scholars, as opposed to the divine texts.
النصوص (al-nusus): Direct scriptural texts from the Quran and Sunnah that are the primary sources of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which context does the Hanafi school consider mafhum as a valid proof?
A) In direct Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths
B) In jurisprudential narrations (riwayat) and rational causes
C) In all texts without exception
D) Only in the statements of endowers (waqifin)

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must not derive rulings from the mafhum al-mukhalafah of a Quranic verse or hadith but may use mafhum from narrations and rational causes, provided it is mafhum al-muwafaqah.

6 min
The Authority of Mafhum: When Implied Meaning is Valid in Hanafi Fatwa
Explanation of Hanafi positions on mafhum as evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on mafhum, apply the restriction to non-divine speech, and evaluate when a mufti may use implied meaning.

Core Idea
Mafhum is valid in human speech, not in divine speech.

This lesson resolves a classic tension in Hanafi usul: two statements appear contradictory—one rejects mafhum absolutely, the other uses it extensively. The later scholars clarified that the key distinction is the source: divine speech vs. human speech. As a mufti, you must know when to accept implied meaning and when to reject it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Observe that al-Ashbah (book of Judgments) states: 'It is not permissible to argue by implication in the speech of people... as for the implication of a narration, it is a proof.' This seems to contradict the earlier statement that mafhum is not considered only in the words of the Lawgiver.
Al-Ashbah's phrase 'in the speech of people' may cause confusion; the reconciliation follows.
2
Examine the reconciliation by later scholars
Al-Biri cites al-Zahiriyya: 'Arguing by implication is not permissible' as the apparent position (zahir al-riwaya). However, al-Sarakhsi and al-Khassaf used mafhum in al-Siyar al-Kabir and hiyal. The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) settled: mafhum is not hujja in divine speech, but is hujja in human speech and rational matters.
The key is distinguishing the context: Shar'i vs. non-Shar'i.
3
Understand the governing qawida and its exception
The qawida: 'Specification by mention does not indicate the negation of what is other than it' (التخصيص بالذكر لا يدل على نفي ما عداه). This applies to divine speech. Exception: in narrations (riwayat), people's mutual understanding (mafhaim al-nas), and rational matters (ma'qulat), specification does indicate negation.
The exception is a dabt (sub-maxim) that restricts the main qawida.
4
Apply to fatwa practice
When issuing a fatwa on a contract or testimony, a mufti may use mafhum to infer that if a specific condition is mentioned, other conditions are excluded. When deriving a ruling from Quran or Hadith, mafhum cannot be used as evidence.
This prevents misuse of implied meaning in Shari'ah interpretation.

مواضع اعتبار المفهوم

مطلب
في ذكر قولي الحنفية في المفهوم والتطبيق بينهما

فإن قلت : قال في "الأشباه" من كتاب القضاء: "لا يجوز الاحتجاج بالمفهوم في كلام الناس في ظاهر المذهب كالأدلة، وأما مفهوم الرواية فحجة، كما في غاية البيان من الحج". فهذا مخالف لما مر من أنه غير معتبر في كلام الشارع فقط.

قلت : الذي عليه المتأخرون ما قدمناه.

وقال العلامة البيري في شرحه : والذي في "الظهيرية" : "الاحتجاج بالمفهوم لا يجوز". وهو ظاهر المذهب عند علماءنا - رحمهم الله تعالى- وما ذكره محمد في السير الكبير من جواز الاحتجاج بالمفهوم، فذلك خلاف ظاهر الرواية.

قال في حواشي "الكشف" : "رأيت في الفوائد الظهيرية" في باب ما يكره في الصلاة : إن الاحتجاج بالمفهوم يجوز، ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي في السير الكبير، وقال : بنى محمد مسائل السير على الاحتجاج بالمفهوم، وإلى هذا مال الخصاف، وبنى عليه مسائل الحيل". وفي "المصفى" : "التخصيص بالذكر لا يدل على نفي ما عداه، قلنا : التخصيص في الروايات، وفي متفاهم الناس، وفي المعقولات يدل على نفي ما عداه. انتهى من النكاح.

وفي خزانة الروايات : "القيد في الرواية ينفي ما عداه". وفي "السراجية" : أما في متفاهم الناس من الأخبارات، فإن تخصيص الشيء بالذكر يدل على نفي ما عداه كذا ذكره السرخسي. اهـ. أقول : الظاهر أن العمل على ما في "السير" كما اختاره الخصاف في الحيل، ولم نر من خالفه. والله تعالى أعلم. انتهى كلام البيري. أي إن العمل على جواز الاحتجاج بالمفهوم، لكن لا مطلقا، بل في غير كلام الشارع كما علمت مما قررناه، وإلا فالذي رأيته في السير الكبير...
Places of considering the implication (mafhum). Section: Mentioning the two statements of the Hanafis regarding mafhum and reconciliation between them. If you say: He said in al-Ashbah from the book of Judgments: 'It is not permissible to argue by implication in the speech of people according to the apparent position, like evidence. As for the implication of a narration (riwaya), it is a proof, as in Ghayat al-Bayan from the chapter of Hajj.' This contradicts what has passed—that it is not considered only in the words of the Lawgiver. I say: The position of the later scholars is what we have presented. Al-Allama al-Biri said in his commentary: 'What is in al-Zahiriyya: Arguing by implication is not permissible. That is the apparent position of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them). And what Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir about the permissibility of arguing by implication—that is contrary to the manifest transmission (zahir al-riwaya).' He said in the marginalia of al-Kashf: 'I saw in al-Fawaid al-Zahiriyya, in the chapter of what is disliked in prayer: arguing by implication is permissible. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned it in al-Siyar al-Kabir, and he said: Muhammad built the issues of al-Siyar on arguing by implication, and al-Khassaf inclined to this and built on it the issues of legal stratagems (hiyal).' In al-Mustafa: 'Specification by mention does not indicate the negation of what is other than it. We say: Specification (takhsis) in narrations, in people's mutual understanding, and in rational matters indicates the negation of what is other than it. End from the chapter of Nikah.' In Khizanat al-Riwayat: 'The restriction (qayd) in a narration negates what is other than it.' In al-Sirajiyya: 'As for people's mutual understanding of statements, specifying a thing by mention indicates the negation of what is other than it. Thus al-Sarakhsi mentioned it.' End. I say: The apparent is that practice is upon what is in al-Siyar, as al-Khassaf chose in the hiyal, and we have not seen anyone who opposed him. And Allah knows best. End of al-Biri's speech. That is: practice is on the permissibility of arguing by implication, but not absolutely; rather, in other than the words of the Lawgiver, as you have known from what we have established. Otherwise, what I have seen in al-Siyar al-Kabir...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical rule for muftis: can implication (mafhum) be used as evidence when issuing fatwas? The default Hanafi position (zahir al-riwaya) is that mafhum is not a proof in the words of the Lawgiver (the Qur'an and Sunnah), because divine speech may carry hidden meanings. However, in the speech of people—such as contracts, testimonies, or waqf deeds—mafhum is valid. The text reconciles an apparent contradiction: some Hanafi works (like al-Siyar al-Kabir by Imam Muhammad) used mafhum extensively, while others (like al-Ashbah) reject it. Al-Biri, quoting al-Sarakhsi and al-Khassaf, explains that the later scholars (mutakhkhirun) restrict mafhum to non-divine speech. The key principle is that specification in a narration (e.g., a hadith) does not necessarily negate what is omitted, but specification in people's understanding and rational matters does. Thus, a mufti should not rely on mafhum from the Qur'an or Hadith, but can use it in analyzing legal documents or everyday speech.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning understood from the wording, not the explicit text (mantuq).
التخصيص بالذكر (al-takhsis bi al-dhikr): Specifying something by mentioning it explicitly, which may imply that others are excluded.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The transmitted and relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, as opposed to rare narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only in the words of the Lawgiver (Quran and Sunnah)
B) Only in the speech of people (e.g., contracts, testimonies)
C) In all contexts equally
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A mufti may use mafhum when interpreting contracts and testimonies, but not when deriving rulings from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Status of Mafhum in Lawgiver's Speech vs. People's Speech
Rule with dispute on mafhum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that mafhum is not considered in the Lawgiver's speech but is considered in human speech, and identify when to use it in fatwa.

Core Idea
Mafhum is not a proof in the Quran and Sunnah because divine speech is supremely eloquent; it is a proof only in human speech.

This lesson explains a key distinction in Hanafi usul: the treatment of mafhum (the implication of a restriction) differs between the speech of the Lawgiver (Qur'an and Hadith) and the speech of people. While some earlier Hanafis considered mafhum universally, the later, relied-upon position restricts it to non-Shar'i contexts. Understanding this is crucial for a mufti when deriving rulings from texts and when interpreting contracts or wills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Note that Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Siyar al-Kabir suggests mafhum is a proof even in the Lawgiver's speech, while the later scholars (mutakkhirun) restrict it to human speech.
2
Analyze the later scholars' dalil
The later scholars argue that divine speech is at the highest level of eloquence, so a restriction may have multiple purposes (e.g., indicating the usual case) beyond negating the opposite. They cite the verse about step-daughters (4:23).
The restriction 'under your guardianship' is not a condition but merely mentions the common situation.
3
Understand the distinction
In human speech, custom dictates that restrictions are intended for exclusion. Therefore, mafhum is a proof in contracts, wills, and juristic writings.
The principle 'what is established by custom is like what is established by text' supports this.
4
Apply to a fatwa scenario
When a hadith contains a restriction, do not assume the opposite ruling for other cases. Instead, consider possible other reasons for the restriction. In human speech, treat the restriction as negating the ruling for other cases.
Example: 'Perform wudu with date water' does not imply other water is invalid.

Commentary on the Contract of the Mufti 171. Places of considering the concept (mafhum). People, because what he mentioned in this chapter from the speech of the prince is from the speech of people, not from the speech of the Lawgiver. And this agrees with what passed from al-Ashbah. And apparently [1] that the statement that it is a proof in their speech is the statement of the later scholars as understood from the previous expression of Sharh al-Tahrir. Perhaps their basis for that is what we transmitted earlier [2] from al-Siyar al-Kabir, for it is among the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah, indeed it is the last of them in authorship, so acting upon it is as we presented in the poetic text. The speech of people, and despite that he did not consider it a proof. And the apparent from the speech of Muhammad rahimahullah in al-Siyar al-Kabir is that it is a proof even in the speech of the Lawgiver. And the later scholars among our companions considered it a proof in other than the legislative texts, reconciling between the apparent madhhab and the speech of Muhammad, and distinguishing between the speech of the Lawgiver and other than it, and acting upon the custom of the elite and commoners. (1) His statement: 'And apparently...' This is an application between the two statements by making the first statement for the early scholars and restricting it to the speech of the Lawgiver, and making the second statement for the later scholars and restricting it to other than the speech of the Lawgiver. (2) His statement: 'What we transmitted earlier' means by it the subsidiary issue he mentioned last: 'If the prince's herald said: Whoever wants fodder let him come out under the banner of so-and-so.' Then they have two other bases alluded to in his following speech. The first: The distinction between the speech of the Lawgiver and other; for it is at the highest level of eloquence, so it is possible that a single word occurring in His speech has many benefits difficult for the understanding of eloquent people to grasp, and numerous subtleties that the minds of intelligentsia are incapable of reaching, unlike the speech of others. The second: The custom of people considering it in their speech, and what is established by custom is like what is established by text. Similarly, the custom of jurists and authors has proceeded considering it in the books of Shariah, for they do not intend by the restrictions occurring in their speech except to exclude what contradicts them.

Commentary on the Contract of the Mufti 172. The reason for not considering the concept in the speech of the Lawgiver [Section: On the reason for not considering the concept in the speech of the Lawgiver and considering it in other than it] And the conclusion is that practice now is on considering the concept in other than the speech of the Lawgiver; because specification of something in His speech does not necessarily imply that its benefit is negation of what is other than it, because His speech is a mine of eloquence, so His intent may be otherwise, as in His statement, exalted: 'and your step-daughters who are under your guardianship'; indeed the benefit of the restriction to guardianship is that this is the predominant case regarding step-daughters. As for people's speech, it is devoid of this quality, so one adduces from their speech the implied meaning because that is the customary among them. And it has been explicitly stated in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by text,' and it is close to the statement of jurists: 'What is known is like a stipulated condition.' Consequently, what is established by custom is as if the speaker textually stated it, so it is acted upon. And similarly it is said regarding the concept of narrations; for the scholars, their custom in their books is that they mention restrictions and conditions and the like, drawing attention to excluding what does not contain them. (1) His statement: 'because specification of something' said in al-Talwih: 'The summary of the speech is that the indication of specification by the description of negation of what is other than it is conditional upon certainty that there is no cause for specification except that, and the condition is perpetually absent, so the conditioned is perpetually absent. As for the condition, it is apparent. As for the perpetual absence of the condition, because the benefits of the description are not limited nor restricted, especially in the speech of God the Exalted and the speech of the Messenger, peace be upon him, for it is possible that a single word from them has many benefits that the understanding of intelligentsia is incapable of grasping. And when they are not limited and known, certainty is not achieved of the absence of all except the indication of negation of the ruling regarding what is other than it.' (297/1)
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi rule on considering the implied meaning (mafhum) in the speech of the Lawgiver (the Qur'an and Hadith) versus the speech of people. The author begins by noting that the earlier Hanafi scholars held that mafhum is a proof in all speech, including that of the Lawgiver, based on a statement from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir. However, the later scholars reconciled this with the apparent madhhab by distinguishing between divine and human speech. They argued that divine speech is at the highest level of eloquence, so a restriction may have multiple purposes beyond simply negating the opposite, as illustrated by the verse about step-daughters: the restriction 'under your guardianship' merely reflects the usual situation, not a condition. Therefore, mafhum is not considered in the Qur'an and Sunnah. In contrast, people's speech is not so eloquent, and custom dictates that restrictions are intended for exclusion. Thus, in contracts, wills, and other human statements, mafhum is taken into account. The author cites the principle that 'what is established by custom is like what is established by text' and mentions that jurists follow this in their writings. The passage concludes that the current practice is to consider mafhum only in non-Shar'i speech, as a compromise between the positions of the early and later scholars.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning derived from the restriction of a ruling to a specific description (mafhum al-mukhalafa), i.e., if a ruling is attached to a description, it implies the opposite ruling for cases without that description.
كلام الشارع (kalam al-shari'): The speech of the Lawgiver, i.e., the Qur'an and the words of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) in hadith.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that form the core of the Hanafi school: al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the Lawgiver's speech is not subject to human reasoning.
B) Because the Lawgiver's speech is supremely eloquent, so restrictions may have multiple benefits beyond negation.
C) Because the Quranic verse about step-daughters explicitly denies mafhum.
D) Because Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani rejected mafhum in his later works.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum (implied meaning from restriction) is not a proof in the speech of the Lawgiver, but it is a proof in the speech of people and jurists.

10 min
Mafhum in the Lawgiver's Speech: The Hanafi Distinction
Explains the rule that mafhum is not taken from the Lawgiver's speech but from others' speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when mafhum (implicit meaning) is authoritative in Hanafi fatwa and apply the maxim 'al-thabit bil-'urf kalthabit bil-nass' to justify considering mafhum in non-Lawgiver speech.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, mafhum is not authoritative in divine speech due to eloquence, but custom makes it binding in human speech.

This lesson explains a foundational rule of Hanafi ifta: the difference between the speech of the Lawgiver (Quran and hadith) and the speech of others regarding implicit meaning (mafhum). Because divine speech is supremely eloquent, a qualification does not necessarily negate the opposite; it may be due to the common situation. In contrast, people customarily use qualifications to limit rulings, so mafhum is considered via the maxim 'what is established by custom is like what is established by text.' This lesson will guide you through the proof, the maxim, and its application in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Articulate the Hanafi position: mafhum is not considered in the Lawgiver's speech but is considered in other speech.
The rule is the basis for the entire discussion.
2
Analyze the proof for Lawgiver
Examine the verse on step-daughters and the argument from al-Talwih: since the benefits of divine speech are unlimited, one cannot be certain that a qualification is meant to exclude.
The qualification in 4:23 is due to prevalence, not exclusivity.
3
Apply the custom maxim
Use the maxim 'al-thabit bil-'urf kalthabit bil-nass' to justify considering mafhum in non-Lawgiver speech because custom treats qualifications as exclusive.
This maxim is supported by the sub-maxim 'al-ma'ruf kalmashrut'.
4
Identify the exception
Note that this maxim does not apply to the Lawgiver's speech because custom cannot overrule divine intent; the condition of certainty is always absent.
This is the istithna' in the qawida panel.

(۲۹۷/۱) ذلك القيد ونحوه، وأن حكمه مخالف لحكم المنطوق، وهذا بما شاع وذاع بينهم بلا نكير، ولذا لم ير من صرح بخلافه .
The aspect of not considering implicit meaning in the speech of the Lawgiver. A section on the aspect of not considering implicit meaning in the speech of the Lawgiver and considering it in other than it. The outcome is that practice now is on considering implicit meaning in other than the speech of the Lawgiver; because specifying something in His speech does not necessitate that its benefit is negation of what is other than it, because His speech is the mine of eloquence. It may be that His intent is other than that, as in His statement: 'and your step-daughters who are in your guardianship'; for the benefit of restriction by 'guardianship' is that it is the prevalent in step-daughters. As for the speech of people, it is devoid of this merit, so one infers from their speech based on implicit meaning because it is the customary practice among them. It has been explicitly stated in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by text,' and it is close to the statement of the jurists: 'The known (custom) is like the stipulated condition.' Thus, what is established by custom is as if the speaker explicitly stated it, so it is acted upon. Similarly is said regarding the implicit meaning of narrations; for the scholars' habit in their books is that they mention qualifications and conditions and the like to alert to the exclusion of what does not contain that qualification, (1) His statement: 'because specifying something...' said in al-Talwih: 'The summary of the statement is that the indication of specification by description of negation of what is other than it is conditional upon certainty that there is no cause for specification other than that, and the condition is always absent, so the conditioned is always absent. As for the conditionality, it is apparent. And as for the perpetual absence of the condition, it is because the benefits of the description are unlimited and unconfined, especially in the speech of Allah and the speech of His Messenger, peace be upon him, for it is possible that a single word from them has many benefits that the understanding of intelligent beings cannot grasp. And if they are not known and limited, certainty of the negation of all except the indication of the negation of the ruling from what is other than it is not obtained.' That qualification and its like, and that its ruling is contrary to the ruling of the explicit statement, and this has become widespread among them without any repudiation, and therefore no one has been seen to explicitly contradict it.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage addresses a fundamental rule in Hanafi usul al-ifta: when is implicit meaning (mafhum) considered authoritative? The author explains that the speech of the Lawgiver (Quran and Prophetic hadith) is treated differently from the speech of ordinary people. Because divine speech is supremely eloquent, a qualification (like 'who are in your guardianship' in the verse about step-daughters) does not necessarily negate the ruling for cases lacking that qualification. The qualification may be due to the prevalent situation, not to restrict the ruling. In contrast, people's speech customarily uses qualifications to indicate exceptions, so mafhum is considered. The author supports this with two maxims: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by text' and 'The known (customary) is like the stipulated condition.' These maxims mean that customary practice has the force of an explicit condition. The passage also notes that scholars in their works commonly mention conditions and qualifications to signal that the ruling differs for cases without them, a practice so widespread that none have opposed it. The footnote from al-Talwih provides a deeper logical analysis: for mafhum to negate the opposite, one must be certain that the sole purpose of the qualification is to limit the ruling; but in divine speech, the benefits are unlimited, so such certainty is impossible.

Key Terms
مفهوم (mafhum): The implied meaning derived from a qualification in speech, typically that the ruling is exclusive to the mentioned case and its opposite is negated.
منطوق (manṭūq): The explicit, stated meaning of a text, from which the ruling is directly derived.
ثابت بالعرف (thābit bi al-'urf): Something that is established through customary practice, treated as if explicitly stated in the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, why is implicit meaning (mafhum) not considered in the speech of the Lawgiver (Quran and hadith)?
A) Because the Lawgiver's speech is customarily used for explicit commands only.
B) Because the Lawgiver's speech is eloquent and may have multiple benefits, so qualifications are not necessarily exclusive.
C) Because the Lawgiver never uses qualifications.
D) Because the Sahibayn opposed considering mafhum in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rejects automatic application of mafhum in divine speech but accepts it in human speech via the custom maxim 'al-thabit bil-'urf kalthabit bil-nass'.

Mafhum in Fiqh Narrations: Dominant but Not Absolute
Discusses the principle that mafhum is predominant but not absolute, with an example from al-Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a qualification in a fiqh narration is accidental (ittifaqi) or precautionary (ihtirazi) and apply the rule that mafhum is predominant but not absolute in Hanafi fatwa.

Core Idea
Not every qualification in fiqh texts creates mafhum; many are incidental (ittifaqi) and do not exclude other cases.

This passage from the works of rasm al-mufti deals with a crucial distinction for muftis: when does a qualifying phrase in a fiqh narration generate an exclusive meaning (mafhum), and when is it merely incidental? The author establishes that considering mafhum is predominant (aghlabi) in fiqh works, but it is not universal. We will examine the classic example from al-Hidayah regarding washing hands upon waking for wudu', and see how the majority of Hanafi scholars treat the qualification as incidental, while a minority view (Kardari) treats it as precautionary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualification
In the al-Hidayah text, the phrase 'when he awakens from his sleep' qualifies the sunna of washing hands before entering the vessel.
2
Classify the qualification
Determine if it is ittifaqi (accidental) or ihtirazi (precautionary). The majority says ittifaqi; Kardari says ihtirazi.
3
Apply the principle
If ittifaqi, the ruling (sunna) applies to both the awakened and others. If ihtirazi, it applies only to the awakened.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The majority view is relied upon in fatwa, supported by the refutation (naqd) from al-Ayni regarding the person who wakes after istinja.
5
Generalize the rule
The mufti must be cautious: mafhum is predominant but not absolute; each qualification must be examined against the underlying wisdom (illah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب اعتبار المفهوم في الروايات الفقهية أكثرى نعم ! ذلك أغلبى، كما عزاه القهستاني في شرح النقاية " إلى حدود النهاية". ومن غير الغالب قول "الهداية" : "وسنن الطهارة : غسل اليدين قبل إدخالهما الإناء، إذا استيقظ المتوضئ من نومه " ؛ فإن التقييد بالاستيقاظ اتفاقی، وقع تبركا بلفظ الحديث، فإن السنة تشمل المستيقظ وغيره عند الأكثرين . وقيل : إنه احترازى) ؛ لإخراج غير المستيقظ، وإليه مال شمس الأئمة الكردري . (۱) قوله : "وقيل : إنه احترازي" قال العلامة العيني رحمه الله تعالى : " وعن شمس الأئمة الكردري كلمة الشرط تجرى على حقيقتها حتى لم يسن إذا لم يستيقظ ، وقيد في "الإيضاح " و في "شرح مختصر الكرخي " وسائر شروح القدوري " : أن كونه سنة للمستيقظ من نومه فحسب؛ لأن النوم مظنة واليد طوافة على البدن، فلعلها أن تقع على موضع النجاسة، لكن هذا مردود بمن قام مستنجيا بالماء؛ فإنه لا حاجة له إلى غسل اليدين أولا . (البناية : ۱۳۲/۱)
A section: Considering implicit meaning in fiqh narrations is more common. Yes! That is predominant, as al-Qahistani attributed to 'the limits of al-Nihaya'. And from what is not predominant is the statement of al-Hidayah: 'And the sunan (recommended acts) of purification: washing the hands before inserting them into the vessel, when the wudu' performer awakens from his sleep.' For the qualification by awakening is accidental, occurring as a blessing of the wording of the hadith. For the sunna includes the awakened and others according to the majority. And it was said that it is precautionary, to exclude the one who is not awakened, and Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari inclined to this. (1) His statement: 'And it was said that it is precautionary.' al-Allama al-Ayni, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And from Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari: the word of condition is taken according to its literal meaning so that it is not sunna if he does not awake. And it is restricted in al-Idah and in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi and all the commentaries of al-Quduri: that it is only sunna for the one who wakes from his sleep because sleep is a place of likelihood and the hand roams over the body, perhaps it lands on a place of impurity. But this is refuted by the one who gets up after using water for istinja; for he has no need to wash his hands first. (al-Binaya: 1/132)'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage discusses the extent to which mafhum is applied in fiqh narrations. The author states that considering mafhum is predominant (aghlabi) in these works. However, it is not universal; there are examples where the qualification is not intended to exclude other cases. The example from al-Hidayah is the sunna of washing hands upon waking from sleep. The text of al-Hidayah restricts it to when one wakes up, but the majority of scholars hold that this qualification is incidental (ittifaqi)—it came in the hadith wording but does not restrict the ruling; the sunna applies both to the awakened and others. Others, including Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari, argue that the qualification is precautionary (ihtirazi) to exclude the non-awakened, meaning the sunna is only for the awakened. The footnote from al-Binaya provides the reasoning of al-Kardari: the condition is taken literally, so if one does not wake, it is not sunna. But this is refuted by the case of someone who wakes up after using water to clean himself; there is no need to wash hands first, indicating the wisdom behind the rule is not exclusive to awakening. This illustrates that even in fiqh works, mafhum is not always applied; the context and underlying reasoning matter.

Key Terms
اتفاقی (ittifāqī): Accidental or incidental; a qualification that occurred in the wording but is not intended to limit the ruling.
احترازى (iḥtirāzī): Precautionary; a qualification intended to exclude other cases and thus create mafhum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the example from al-Hidayah about washing hands upon waking demonstrate?
A) That mafhum is always considered in fiqh narrations.
B) That the qualification 'upon waking' is precautionary (ihtirazi).
C) That the dominant view considers the qualification incidental, so mafhum is not applied here.
D) That al-Kardari's view is unanimously accepted.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum is predominant in fiqh narrations but not absolute; a mufti must distinguish between accidental (ittifaqi) and precautionary (ihtirazi) qualifications.

Explicit Text (Sarih) Takes Precedence over Mafhum
States the rule that explicit text is given priority over implicit meaning when they conflict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that an explicit text (ṣarīḥ) overrides an implicit meaning (mafhūm) when they conflict, and identify when a mafhum remains authoritative.

Core Idea
Explicit text always prevails over implicit meaning — clarity overrides inference.

In legal interpretation, not all textual indications carry equal weight. The Hanafi school recognizes that while derived implications (mafahim) can be used to deduce rulings, they are subordinate to direct, unambiguous statements. This lesson establishes the hierarchy: when a clear text conflicts with an inferred meaning, the clear text takes precedence. This rule prevents subjective interpretation from overriding the explicit command of the Lawgiver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of textual indication
Understand that a text can be explicit by direct wording (sarih) or implicit by implication (mafhum). Both are considered evidence, but with different strength.
Mafhum includes both mafhum muwafaqa (inline meaning) and mafhum mukhalafa (opposite meaning), but the rule applies broadly.
2
Recognize the default authority of mafhum
The author states that mafhum is authoritative (hujja) as long as it does not contradict an explicit text. This means mafhum is valid evidence, but conditional on non-conflict.
In Hanafi usul, mafhum is accepted in non-divine speech (e.g., fatwa) and in some Quranic contexts, but always subordinate to sarih.
3
Apply the rule of precedence
When a sarih and a mafhum lead to conflicting rulings, the sarih must be followed and the mafhum abandoned. This is based on the principle that the clearer evidence overrides the less clear.
This is a hierarchical rule: sarih > mafhum, regardless of the strength of the inference.
4
Test with a fiqh scenario
Consider a hadith explicitly stating 'The killer does not inherit.' A mafhum from another text might suggest accidental killing allows inheritance. The explicit text overrides, so no inheritance for the killer.
Always prioritize direct textual commands over derived implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب الصريح مقدم على المفهوم وقولى: "ما لم يخالف لصريح ثبتا" أى إن المفهوم حجة على ما قررناه إذا لم يخالف صريحًا، فإن الصريح مقدم على المفهوم، كما صرح به
A section: The explicit is given precedence over the implicit. And my statement: 'As long as it does not contradict an explicit that is established' means that the implicit meaning is authoritative according to what we have established if it does not contradict an explicit text. For the explicit is given precedence over the implicit, as explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This short passage establishes a fundamental rule in usul al-ifta: when there is a conflict between an explicit text (sarih) and an implicit meaning (mafhum), the explicit text takes precedence. The author quotes from his own work: 'as long as it does not contradict an explicit that is established.' That is, the mafhum that we have argued is authoritative in non-Lawgiver speech and in fiqh works remains authoritative only as long as it does not clash with a clear, explicit statement. If it does, the explicit statement must be followed. This principle is common sense in legal interpretation: the clearer evidence overrides the less clear. The phrase 'as clearly stated' indicates that this rule is well-known and has been explicitly mentioned by earlier scholars.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit, unambiguous text that directly states a ruling.
مفهوم (mafhūm): Implicit meaning derived from textual context, such as from a qualification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this rule, what happens if a mafhum (implicit meaning) contradicts a sarih (explicit text)?
A) The mafhum is given precedence because it is derived from the text.
B) The sarih is given precedence over the mafhum.
C) Both are considered equally and a mujtahid may choose.
D) The later text abrogates the earlier one.

Key Takeaway
When an explicit text conflicts with an implied meaning, the explicit text must be followed unconditionally.

The Prevalent Nature of Mafhum in Hanafi Narrations
Rasm rule: mafhum is prevalent but not absolute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that mafhum is prevalent but not absolute, and apply it by distinguishing between incidental (ittifaqi) and restrictive (ihtirazi) textual conditions.

Core Idea
Mafhum is prevalent in Hanafi fiqh but not universally binding—some restrictions are incidental.

Why do Hanafi fiqh texts sometimes use conditional phrases that appear to limit a ruling, yet the school's established position treats the condition as non-restrictive? This lesson unpacks a key rasm al-mufti principle: the prevalent nature of mafhum (implied meaning) in Hanafi narrations. Through the classic example of washing hands after sleep, we explore how the majority of Hanafi scholars treat certain textual restrictions as incidental (ittifaqi) rather than legally operative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about mafhum
The passage states that considering mafhum (implied meaning) is prevalent (akthari) among Hanafis, but not absolute. This is the core rasm rule: mafhum is relied upon generally but may be overridden by other evidence.
2
Examine the example from al-Hidayah
The phrase 'when the one performing wudu awakens from his sleep' seems to restrict the sunnah of washing hands to only those who wake. Yet the majority consider this restriction incidental, not legally intended.
The sunnah includes both awakened and non-awakened.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari argued the condition is restrictive (ihtirazi), limiting the sunnah to the awakened. His evidence is that conditional words should be taken at face value.
Al-Kardari's position is minority.
4
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
Al-Ayni refutes al-Kardari using the case of one who already washed after istinja—if the condition were real, that person would need to wash again, but they do not. This shows the condition is incidental, not legally binding.
The refutation uses a counterexample from practice.
5
Apply the rasm rule to other texts
A mufti must check whether a textual restriction is incidental or truly restrictive. Look for evidence in practice, consensus, or stronger proofs that may override the apparent mafhum.
This guides fatwa-giving.

Explanation of the Contract of the Mufti's Code: 173 The consideration of the concept in narrations is prevalent. That restriction and the like, and that its ruling is contrary to the ruling of the expressed text, and this is widespread among them without denial, hence no one is seen explicitly opposing it. A Request: The consideration of the concept in jurisprudential narrations is prevalent. Yes! It is predominant, as attributed to al-Qahistani in his commentary on al-Niqayah 'to the limits of al-Nihayah'. Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule discussion about the nature of mafhum (implied meaning) in Hanafi fiqh narrations. The author states that considering mafhum is prevalent among Hanafis, but not absolute. The example from al-Hidayah about washing hands after sleep illustrates that some textual restrictions are incidental (ittifaqi) and not intended for legal specification. The footnote by al-Ayni explains al-Kardari's position that the condition is real, but refutes it by citing the case of one who already washed after istinja, showing the condition is not truly restrictive. The rule guides muftis to be cautious when interpreting implied meanings.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning or inferred ruling from a text, as opposed to the explicit wording (al-mantuq).
المنطوق (al-mantuq): The explicit wording of a text, carrying a direct ruling.
اتفاقي (ittifaqi): Incidental, not legally intentional; a condition that appears in a text but is not meant to create a restriction.
احترازي (ihtirazi): Restrictive; a condition intended to exclude certain cases from the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mafhum in Hanafi narrations is prevalent but not absolute—some textual conditions are incidental and do not restrict the ruling.

Explicit Text Takes Precedence over Implied Meaning
Establishing the priority of explicit text over mafhum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-sarih muqaddam ala al-mafhum' to distinguish when an implied meaning (mafhum) is valid or overridden in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
When an explicit text contradicts an implied meaning, the explicit text prevails.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim of Hanafi usul al-fiqh crucial for muftis: the explicit text (sarih) always takes precedence over any implied meaning (mafhum). The source passage explains that mafhum al-mukhalafa is a valid proof only if it does not contradict an explicit text, and outlines six conditions for its acceptance. Mastering this maxim prevents overextension of implied meanings and ensures fatwa is grounded in the clearest textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Identify the maxim: 'al-sarih muqaddam ala al-mafhum'. Determine its rank as a wusta (intermediate) universal maxim governing all fiqh chapters.
2
Understand the scope
Recognize that the maxim applies whenever there is a conflict between an explicit text and an implied meaning derived from the same or another text. The implied is only considered if no explicit text contradicts it.
The maxim does not negate mafhum altogether; it prioritizes sarih.
3
Work through the furu' example
Apply the maxim to the classic Hanafi example from al-Hidayah about washing hands after sleep: the explicit statement 'if he awakens' is merely incidental, so the implied restriction (only after awakening) is overridden by the general sunnah of washing hands before prayers.
The explicit text in question is the general command to wash hands; the phrase about awakening is not a true condition.
4
Identify the istithna' exceptions
Note that the maxim itself is a dabt (restriction) to the rule that mafhum is considered evidence. The six conditions for mafhum al-mukhalafa (listed in the footnote) serve as additional safeguards.
The only case where mafhum might prevail is if the explicit text is weak or inapplicable, but this is rare.
5
Apply in fatwa
When deriving a fatwa, always check whether any explicit text (Quran, Hadith, established ijma) speaks directly to the issue. If so, that explicit text determines the ruling; any implied meaning to the contrary is invalid. Only if there is no explicit text may mafhum be considered, and subject to the six conditions.
This step is critical for muftis to avoid issuing weak or contradictory rulings.

Source Text

مطلب
الصريح مقدم على المفهوم
وقولى: "ما لم يخالف لصريح ثبتا" أى إن المفهوم حجة على ما قررناه إذا لم يخالف صريحًا، فإن الصريح مقدم على المفهوم، كما صرح به
(۱) قوله : "إذا لم يأت صريح بخلافه" اعلم أن القائلين باعتبار مفهوم المخالفة قيدوه بشروط سنة : أن لا يعارضه ما هو أقوى منه كالمنطوق ومفهوم الموافقة، وأن لا يعود على أصله الذى هو المنطوق بالإبطال، وأن لا يكون المنطوق خرج جواباً عن سؤال متعلق بحكم خاص، وأن لا يكون قد خرج مخرج الأغلب ومراعاة الواقع، وأن لا يكون للقيد الذي قيد به النص فائدة أخرى غير نفى الحكم عن المسكوت، مثل الترغيب، أو الترهيب، أو التنفير أو التفخيم، أو تأكيد الحال، أو الامتنان ونحو ذلك، وأن يذكر مستقلا لا تبعا ، فلو فقد شرط من هذه الشروط لا يعتبر . راجع : الإرشاد للشوكاني : ٥٩/٢-٦١ .
والله تعالى أعلم
A Request: Explicit text takes precedence over the implied meaning. My statement: 'As long as it does not contradict an explicit text that is established' means that the implied meaning is evidence according to what we have established if it does not contradict an explicit text, for the explicit takes precedence over the implied, as stated by al-Tarsusi and others, and mentioned by usul scholars in preferring evidences. Those who consider the implied meaning of opposition restrict it with six conditions: that it is not opposed by something stronger like the explicit text or the implied meaning of agreement, and that it does not invalidate its origin which is the explicit text, and that the explicit text did not come as an answer to a question about a specific ruling, and that it did not come as a predominant consideration, and that the qualification has no other benefit besides negating the ruling from the unmentioned, such as encouragement, intimidation, repulsion, magnification, emphasis, favor, etc., and that it is mentioned independently not as a dependent. If any condition is missing, it is not considered. Reference: al-Irshad by al-Shawkani 2/59-61. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial qawida (legal maxim) for ifta: the explicit text (sarih) takes precedence over implied meaning (mafhum). The author is commenting on his own statement that mafhum is valid only if it does not contradict an explicit text. The footnote outlines the six conditions for the validity of mafhum al-mukhalafa (implied meaning of opposition) as a proof. The maxim serves as a guard for muftis to avoid overextending implied meanings when clear textual rulings exist. This is a core principle in Hanafi and other schools' methodologies.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-sarih): The explicit wording of a text that clearly indicates a ruling without ambiguity.
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning derived from a text, which may be of agreement or opposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the ruling when an explicit text contradicts an implied meaning (mafhum)?
A) The implied meaning is given priority
B) The explicit text takes precedence
C) The weaker of the two is followed
D) They are reconciled

Key Takeaway
Explicit text (sarih) always beats implied meaning (mafhum); the implied is only valid if no explicit text contradicts it.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Definition and Authority of Custom ('Urf) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines custom and establishes its proof through Quran, Hadith, and scholarly principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim that custom (‘urf) can specify general texts and determine the meaning of absolute expressions, and evaluate the conditions under which custom is legally valid.

Core Idea
Custom is a valid source of law when it is predominant or continuous, and it can even override literal meanings.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi doctrine of ‘urf (custom) as a supplementary source of law. The passage defines ‘urf, establishes its proof from the Quran and Sunnah, and lays out the key principles and conditions for its application. By the end, you will understand how custom operates in contracts, maintenance, and other areas, and why the maxim 'tutraku al-haqiqah bi dalalat al-istimal wa al-'adah' is central to Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of ‘urf
Locate the term ‘urf in the passage and note its definition: 'what people have mutually agreed upon and settled in their customs of speech or action'. Also note the distinction between ‘urf and ‘adah.
The passage does not give an explicit definition, but the context defines it as conventional practice.
2
Examine the dalil naqli
Identify the Quranic verses (nineteen instances of 'bi al-ma'ruf') and the hadith 'What the Muslims see as good is good in the sight of Allah'. Understand that these texts command following customary practice in specific matters.
The hadith is noted as weak in marfu' form but accepted as mawquf and authentic in meaning.
3
Extract the four principles (usul arba‘ah)
From the text, list the four ways custom operates: (1) determining word meanings, (2) interpreting contracts, (3) change of rulings with time, (4) fatwa according to people's custom in cases of necessity.
These principles are foundational for applying custom in fatwa.
4
Understand the central maxim
Examine the maxim 'tutraku al-haqiqah bi dalalat al-istimal wa al-'adah'. Note that it means the literal meaning is abandoned when there is a customary usage that is continuous or predominant.
This maxim is a wusta (intermediate) qa‘ida governing interpretation.
5
Apply the conditions and furuat
Note the condition that custom must be continuous (mutarid) or predominant (ghalib). Apply to the sale of dirhams/dinars and maintenance examples. Check if the custom is widespread and not shared.
If custom is shared (not predominant), it cannot restrict the absolute meaning.

Source Text
